<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-966762f2d4&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:41:20Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>It’s also worth noting that since the Go &lt;i>net.http.Client&lt;/i> is leveraged, configuration of this attack type is done via the target directive, and will support anything that the (well-rounded and very capable) library does. This includes HTTPS, redirection following, domain resolution, HTTP auth headers, etc. In the attack command in Figure 32 the move to &lt;i>https://&lt;/i> causes the built in library to utilize TLS, target port 443, and since we included the &lt;i>user:pass@&lt;/i> in the target directive, the traffic will also include a &lt;i>Authorization&lt;/i>: &lt;i>Basic dXNlcjpwYXNz&lt;/i> header as well.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>As of this writing, it appears that the attack requests method is hard coded and therefore limited to HTTP GET requests … for now.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>It’s also worth noting that since the Go <i>net.http.Client</i> is leveraged, configuration of this attack type is done via the target directive, and will support anything that the (well-rounded and very capable) library does. This includes HTTPS, redirection following, domain resolution, HTTP auth headers, etc. In the attack command in Figure 32 the move to <i>https://</i> causes the built in library to utilize TLS, target port 443, and since we included the <i>user:pass@</i> in the target directive, the traffic will also include a <i>Authorization</i>: <i>Basic dXNlcjpwYXNz</i> header as well.</p>
<p>As of this writing, it appears that the attack requests method is hard coded and therefore limited to HTTP GET requests … for now.</p>

</div>

<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-bf53966a18&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:41:27Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>Attack type 1: &lt;i>udp_flood&lt;/i>&lt;/h3>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>Attack type 1: <i>udp_flood</i></h3>

</div>

<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-78e787db17&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:41:40Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The &lt;i>udp_flood&lt;/i> attack command structure requires all fields outlined previously, even if the option supplied doesn’t exist (Figure 33). In analysis and testing, we were able to identify a single option field, which is used to control the targeted port. If no option is passed, the binary fails to parse the attack command; in some cases, passed values via this field even crash the bot.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The <i>udp_flood</i> attack command structure requires all fields outlined previously, even if the option supplied doesn’t exist (Figure 33). In analysis and testing, we were able to identify a single option field, which is used to control the targeted port. If no option is passed, the binary fails to parse the attack command; in some cases, passed values via this field even crash the bot.</p>

</div>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-7274dd1774&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T20:26:32Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Fig. 33: Attack command structure for a udp_flood attack&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-6d593c5356&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:43:04Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This attack command looks slightly different than the&lt;i> http_flood&lt;/i> variant, mostly due to the &lt;i>UDP_OPTIONS&lt;/i> value passed (&lt;i>1531337&lt;/i>) in the final parameter. This parameter controls the targeted port that the UDP packets will be sent to. The value is actually three parts, the first part is the parameter type (&lt;i>1&lt;/i>), the second is the length (&lt;i>5&lt;/i>) of the value, and the third is the value itself (&lt;i>31337&lt;/i>).&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>It seems like this fourth parameter is required for command parsing, but the value can be discarded, and if no port value is supplied here, the binary will default to UDP/80 as the target port of the attack. Initially, there was an assumption that since this data is passed in this manner, we would find other additional configuration parameters for 1-9, but it seems that only the port parameter (1) has any effect on attack traffic exiting the bot.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This attack command looks slightly different than the<i> http_flood</i> variant, mostly due to the <i>UDP_OPTIONS</i> value passed (<i>1531337</i>) in the final parameter. This parameter controls the targeted port that the UDP packets will be sent to. The value is actually three parts, the first part is the parameter type (<i>1</i>), the second is the length (<i>5</i>) of the value, and the third is the value itself (<i>31337</i>). </p>
<p>It seems like this fourth parameter is required for command parsing, but the value can be discarded, and if no port value is supplied here, the binary will default to UDP/80 as the target port of the attack. Initially, there was an assumption that since this data is passed in this manner, we would find other additional configuration parameters for 1-9, but it seems that only the port parameter (1) has any effect on attack traffic exiting the bot.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-9d41788009&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:43:39Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The screenshot in Figure 34 shows the setup of the &lt;i>udp_flood&lt;/i> socket. It utilizes Go’s &lt;i>net &lt;/i>library using &lt;i>net.Dial&lt;/i> to create the UDP socket. It then creates 512 workers, which share the socket, each running in a loop pushing data over the socket until the duration timer sends them a kill command via a shared channel. The UDP packets (Figure 35) that exit the bot are very large (65,549 bytes per packet in total), and are likely to arrive fragmented to victims across the internet . This size is hard coded within the binary and not under control of the attacker on a per-attack basis.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The screenshot in Figure 34 shows the setup of the <i>udp_flood</i> socket. It utilizes Go’s <i>net </i>library using <i>net.Dial</i> to create the UDP socket. It then creates 512 workers, which share the socket, each running in a loop pushing data over the socket until the duration timer sends them a kill command via a shared channel. The UDP packets (Figure 35) that exit the bot are very large (65,549 bytes per packet in total), and are likely to arrive fragmented to victims across the internet . This size is hard coded within the binary and not under control of the attacker on a per-attack basis.</p>

</div>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-51d0fa763c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:43:56Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The 65,507 bytes packed into the UDP data segment are all null bytes (Figure 36).The IP ID header does increment sequentially during the attack and, although the source port is ephemeral, it remains static during the duration of the flood. This is all likely a side effect of &lt;i>net.Dial &lt;/i>managing the UDP socket across the entire attacker worker pool. One other thing to note is the failing check sums on the UDP data.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The 65,507 bytes packed into the UDP data segment are all null bytes (Figure 36).The IP ID header does increment sequentially during the attack and, although the source port is ephemeral, it remains static during the duration of the flood. This is all likely a side effect of <i>net.Dial </i>managing the UDP socket across the entire attacker worker pool. One other thing to note is the failing check sums on the UDP data.</p>

</div>

<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="This is all likely a side effect of net.Dial managing the UDP socket across the entire attacker worker pool. One other thing to note is the failing check sums on the UDP data." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-6e270355c0&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T21:41:03Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;This is all likely a side effect of net.Dial managing the UDP socket across the entire attacker worker pool. One other thing to note is the failing check sums on the UDP data.&#34;,&#34;xdm:linkURL&#34;:&#34;/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure36.png&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;8f68dad1-65ac-4250-af5b-586203f37dff&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T21:03:53Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure36.png&#34;}}}">
    
      <a class="cmp-image__link" href="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure36.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" data-cmp-hook-image="link">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/figure36.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="This is all likely a side effect of net.Dial managing the UDP socket across the entire attacker worker pool. One other thing to note is the failing check sums on the UDP data."/>
        
        
      </a>
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 36: Verbose packet capture detail  of UDP attack packet</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-3c1fc1ac33&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T19:17:44Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>How did they measure up?&lt;/h3>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>How did they measure up?</h3>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-da731af186&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-16T16:44:41Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>In order to benchmark these two attack methods, we ran two 10-second attacks, one for each method carving just the attack flows off the wire into packet captures. We were then able to see the overall size of traffic generated per attack type.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The &lt;i>http_flood&lt;/i> generated 3.4 MB of packet capture data and pushed 20,430 HTTP requests. The request sizes ranged from 484 to 589 bytes per request, with sizes varying mostly due to randomization of User-Agent and Cookie header data. These request packet lengths will also be impacted by the inclusion of additional headers (e.g., Authorization Basic data), URL path and GET parameter padding, and inclusion of TLS, so take them with a grain of salt.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>It’s worth pointing out that the server targeted during this attack event was also very simple and single threaded; it’s possible if the attack was directed at a server more capable of responding faster to the worker pool, these numbers could go up.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The &lt;i>udp_flood&lt;/i> generated 6,733 packets for a total of 421 MB of packet capture data over the wire. There isn’t much else that’s interesting about this attack: it is volumetric in nature and seems to do a decent job of pushing volume. As stated previously, due to the size of the packets generated by this attack, victims will likely see a deluge of fragments during real-world attack events.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Using our 10-second sample sets and a theorized size of the botnet, we can begin estimating attack sizing. If the botnet contained just 1,000 nodes, the resulting UDP flood would weigh in at around 336 Gbps per second. With 10,000 nodes (roughly 6.9% of the size of Mirai at its peak), the UDP flood would weigh in at more than 3.3 Tbps. The HTTP flood at 1,000 nodes would generate roughly 2.7 Gbps and more than 2 Mrps. With 10,000 nodes, those numbers jump to 27 Gbps delivering 20.4 Mrps.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>These theorized capabilities obviously don’t take into account the different kinds of servers that would be participating, their respective bandwidth and hardware capabilities, etc., but you get the picture. Let’s hope that the HinataBot authors move onto new hobbies before we have to deal with their botnet at any real scale.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>In order to benchmark these two attack methods, we ran two 10-second attacks, one for each method carving just the attack flows off the wire into packet captures. We were then able to see the overall size of traffic generated per attack type. </p>
<p>The <i>http_flood</i> generated 3.4 MB of packet capture data and pushed 20,430 HTTP requests. The request sizes ranged from 484 to 589 bytes per request, with sizes varying mostly due to randomization of User-Agent and Cookie header data. These request packet lengths will also be impacted by the inclusion of additional headers (e.g., Authorization Basic data), URL path and GET parameter padding, and inclusion of TLS, so take them with a grain of salt. </p>
<p>It’s worth pointing out that the server targeted during this attack event was also very simple and single threaded; it’s possible if the attack was directed at a server more capable of responding faster to the worker pool, these numbers could go up.</p>
<p>The <i>udp_flood</i> generated 6,733 packets for a total of 421 MB of packet capture data over the wire. There isn’t much else that’s interesting about this attack: it is volumetric in nature and seems to do a decent job of pushing volume. As stated previously, due to the size of the packets generated by this attack, victims will likely see a deluge of fragments during real-world attack events.</p>
<p>Using our 10-second sample sets and a theorized size of the botnet, we can begin estimating attack sizing. If the botnet contained just 1,000 nodes, the resulting UDP flood would weigh in at around 336 Gbps per second. With 10,000 nodes (roughly 6.9% of the size of Mirai at its peak), the UDP flood would weigh in at more than 3.3 Tbps. The HTTP flood at 1,000 nodes would generate roughly 2.7 Gbps and more than 2 Mrps. With 10,000 nodes, those numbers jump to 27 Gbps delivering 20.4 Mrps.</p>
<p>These theorized capabilities obviously don’t take into account the different kinds of servers that would be participating, their respective bandwidth and hardware capabilities, etc., but you get the picture. Let’s hope that the HinataBot authors move onto new hobbies before we have to deal with their botnet at any real scale.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-90a6d4e898&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-15T19:17:58Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Conclusion&lt;/h2>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Conclusion</h2>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-a391e02a93&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-03-17T18:38:29Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>HinataBot is the latest example of the evolving threat landscape, particularly in relation to botnets. Malware authors are continuing to innovate their use of implementation methods, languages, and distribution methods. By leaning on older, proven techniques, such as those used within Mirai, attackers can focus more on curating pieces that evade detection,&amp;nbsp; continuously evolve, and add new functionality.&amp;nbsp;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>By continuing to explore and analyze evolving threats such as HinataBot, we can better understand the tactics, techniques, and procedures of attackers to develop more robust defenses against them. The HinataBot family relies on old vulnerabilities and brute forcing weak passwords for distribution. This is yet another example of why strong password and patching policies are more critical than ever. Attackers are always looking for low-hanging fruit with high return on investment, so making it more difficult for attacks to be successful helps significantly in keeping your environment and the internet safe.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This is likely just the beginning for HinataBot. The Akamai SIRT will continue to monitor its evolution over time and report new findings when relevant.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Akamai customers are protected from the two attack capabilities this botnet supports.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Akamai mitigates non-HTTP attacks transparently at the edge, including UDP, TCP, and ICMP floods.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Akamai App &amp;amp; API Protector mitigates L7 web application attacks like this automatically via Akamai Client Reputation, Rate Controls, Akamai Bot Manager, and web application firewall rules.&lt;br>\r\n&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>Should you have additional questions about protections from this botnet, please refer to your Akamai account team for further information.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>HinataBot is the latest example of the evolving threat landscape, particularly in relation to botnets. Malware authors are continuing to innovate their use of implementation methods, languages, and distribution methods. By leaning on older, proven techniques, such as those used within Mirai, attackers can focus more on curating pieces that evade detection,  continuously evolve, and add new functionality.  </p>
<p>By continuing to explore and analyze evolving threats such as HinataBot, we can better understand the tactics, techniques, and procedures of attackers to develop more robust defenses against them. The HinataBot family relies on old vulnerabilities and brute forcing weak passwords for distribution. This is yet another example of why strong password and patching policies are more critical than ever. Attackers are always looking for low-hanging fruit with high return on investment, so making it more difficult for attacks to be successful helps significantly in keeping your environment and the internet safe. </p>
<p>This is likely just the beginning for HinataBot. The Akamai SIRT will continue to monitor its evolution over time and report new findings when relevant.</p>
<p>Akamai customers are protected from the two attack capabilities this botnet supports.</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Akamai mitigates non-HTTP attacks transparently at the edge, including UDP, TCP, and ICMP floods.</p>
</li>
<li><p>Akamai App &amp; API Protector mitigates L7 web application attacks like this automatically via Akamai Client Reputation, Rate Controls, Akamai Bot Manager, and web application firewall rules.<br />
</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Should you have additional questions about protections from this botnet, please refer to your Akamai account team for further information.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

  <code data-cmp-hook-prismjs="code" class="lang-bash">rule detect_hinatabot_strings {
    Meta:
                 description = &#34;This rule detects HinataBot binaries.&#34;
        confidence = &#34;high&#34;
    strings:
        $s1 = &#34;HINATA_START_NICE&#34;
        $s2 = &#34;API_CONNECT_BOT&#34;
        $s3 = &#34;Connection to CNC is OK!&#34;
        $s4 = &#34;API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_CHECK&#34;
        $s5 = &#34;API_CONNECTION_SIGNAL_OK&#34;
        $s6 = &#34;API_CONNECTION_ATTACK&#34;
        $s7 = &#34;Hinata already running&#34;
        $s8 = &#34;API_CONNECTION_KILL_ALL&#34;
        $s9 = &#34;hinata_exists&#34;
        $s10 = &#34;hinata_loaded&#34;
        $s11 = &#34;HINATA_&#34;
    condition:
        3 of ($s*)
}</code>

            <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=chad_seaman">Chad Seaman</a>, <a href="/blog?author=larry_cashdollar">Larry Cashdollar</a>, and <a href="/blog?author=allen-west">Allen West</a></p>
            <p class="cmp-blog-author__date">March 16, 2023</p>
        </div>
    </div>

    
        <div class="cmp-blog-author-bio-section">
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__inline-section">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__image-wrapper">
                    <img src="/site/en/images/blog/userpics/userpic-666-100x100.png" alt="Chad Seaman headshot"/>
                </div>
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__info">
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=chad_seaman">Chad Seaman</a></p>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio-wrapper">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio"><p>Chad Seaman is a Principal Security Researcher and Team Lead of Akamai’s Security Intelligence Response Team. He proudly refers to himself as an “Internet Dumpster Diver,” and enjoys looking through the muck and mire he finds there. Chad began his career as a programmer, and after being exposed to security, exploitation, and forensics via breach investigations, security quickly became his preferred work. He now spends his time engulfed in malware investigations, reverse engineering, vulnerability research, DDoS, and cybercrime investigations. He likes flying airplanes, poking holes in paper at a distance, and spending time in nature, preferably in the woods, on a trail, or on a dirt bike.</p>
</div>
            </div>
        </div>
    
        <div class="cmp-blog-author-bio-section">
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__inline-section">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__image-wrapper">
                    <img src="/site/en/images/blog/userpics/larry-cashdollar.jpeg" alt="Larry Cashdollar"/>
                </div>
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__info">
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=larry_cashdollar">Larry Cashdollar</a></p>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio-wrapper">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio"><p>Larry W. Cashdollar has been working in the security field as a vulnerability researcher for more than 20 years and is currently a Principal Security Researcher on the Security Intelligence Response Team at Akamai. He studied computer science at the University of Southern Maine. Larry has documented more than 300 CVEs and has presented his research at BotConf, BSidesBoston, OWASP Rhode Island, and DEF CON. He enjoys the outdoors and rebuilding small engines in his spare time.</p>
</div>
            </div>
        </div>
    
        <div class="cmp-blog-author-bio-section">
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__inline-section">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__image-wrapper">
                    <img src="/site/en/images/blog/userpics/allen-west.jpeg" alt="Allen West"/>
                </div>
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__info">
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__written-by">Written by</p>
                    <p class="cmp-blog-author__name"><a href="/blog?author=allen-west">Allen West</a></p>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio-wrapper">
                <div class="cmp-blog-author__bio"><p>Allen West is a Security Researcher on Akamai's Security Intelligence Response Team who loves investigating threats and building tools. He is currently pursuing his master's degree in Information Security and Assurance from Carnegie Mellon University. He received his undergraduate degree in Cybersecurity from Northeastern University, and he is a Marine Corps veteran. During his free time, Allen loves to travel, hike, swim — anything outdoors and adventurous.</p>
</div>
            </div>
        </div>
